<th scope="row" style="padding-right: 1em;">Seal</th>
<td><a href="/wiki/File:SA_Army_Badge.png" class="image"><img alt="SA Army Badge.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/ba/SA_Army_Badge.png/100px-SA_Army_Badge.png" width="100" height="99" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/ba/SA_Army_Badge.png/150px-SA_Army_Badge.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/ba/SA_Army_Badge.png/200px-SA_Army_Badge.png 2x" data-file-width="219" data-file-height="216" /></a></td>

<p>The <b>South African Army</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Army" title="Army">army</a> of <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, first formed after the <a href="/wiki/Union_of_South_Africa" title="Union of South Africa">Union of South Africa</a> was created in 1910. The South African military evolved within the tradition of frontier warfare fought by <a href="/wiki/Boer_Commando" title="Boer Commando">Boer Commando</a> (<a href="/wiki/Militia" title="Militia">militia</a>) forces, reinforced by the <a href="/wiki/Afrikaner" class="mw-redirect" title="Afrikaner">Afrikaners</a>' historical distrust of large <a href="/wiki/Standing_armies" class="mw-redirect" title="Standing armies">standing armies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-LOCSA_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LOCSA-4">[4]</a></sup> It then fought as part of the wider British effort in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, but afterwards was cut off from its long-standing <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a> ties with the ascension to power of the <a href="/wiki/National_Party_(South_Africa)" title="National Party (South Africa)">National Party</a> in South Africa in 1948. The National Party's opposition to socialism and democracy, and its introduction of the policy of <a href="/wiki/Apartheid" title="Apartheid">apartheid</a>, led to friction with neighbouring states that helped to spark the border wars in <a href="/wiki/South_West_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="South West Africa">South West Africa</a>, now <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a>, from 1966.</p>
<p>The role of the Army was fundamentally changed by the upheavals of the early 1990s and after 1994 the Army became part of the new <a href="/wiki/South_African_National_Defence_Force" title="South African National Defence Force">South African National Defence Force</a>. It is now becoming increasingly involved in <a href="/wiki/Peacekeeping" title="Peacekeeping">peacekeeping</a> efforts in southern Africa, often as part of wider <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a> operations. The Army is composed of roughly 40,100 regular uniformed personnel, augmented by 12,300 reserve force personnel. The rank/age structure of the army, which deteriorated desperately during the 1990s, is greatly improving through the <a href="/wiki/Military_Skills_Development" class="mw-redirect" title="Military Skills Development">Military Skills Development</a> (MSDS) voluntary national service system. Through this system, young healthy members are being inducted into the regular and reserve forces every year.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=South_African_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_South_Africa" title="Military history of South Africa">Military history of South Africa</a></div>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Union_of_South_Africa" title="Union of South Africa">Union of South Africa</a> was formed in 1910, <a href="/wiki/General" class="mw-redirect" title="General">General</a> <a href="/wiki/Jan_Smuts" title="Jan Smuts">Jan Smuts</a>, the Union's first Minister of Defence, placed a high priority on creating a unified military out of the separate armies of the union's four provinces. The Defence Act (No. 13) of 1912 established a <a href="/wiki/Union_Defence_Force_(South_Africa)" title="Union Defence Force (South Africa)">Union Defence Force</a> (UDF) that included a Permanent Force (or standing army) of career soldiers, an <a href="/wiki/Active_Citizen_Force" class="mw-redirect" title="Active Citizen Force">Active Citizen Force</a> of temporary <a href="/wiki/Conscript" class="mw-redirect" title="Conscript">conscripts</a> and volunteers as well as a <a href="/wiki/Cadet" title="Cadet">Cadet</a> organization.<sup id="cite_ref-LCEarlyMil_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LCEarlyMil-5">[5]</a></sup> The 1912 law also obligated all white males between seventeen and sixty years of age to serve in the military, but this was not strictly enforced as there were a large number of volunteers. Instead, half of the white males aged from 17 to 25 were drafted by lots into the ACF. For training purposes, the Union was divided into 15 military districts.<sup id="cite_ref-Dorning_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dorning-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:2</sup></p>
<p>Initially, the Permanent Force consisted of five <a href="/wiki/Regiments" class="mw-redirect" title="Regiments">regiments</a> of the <a href="/w/index.php?title=South_African_Mounted_Riflemen&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="South African Mounted Riflemen (page does not exist)">South African Mounted Riflemen</a> (SAMR), each with a battery of <a href="/wiki/Artillery" title="Artillery">artillery</a> attached. Dorning says that '..the SAMR was in reality a military constabulary similar to the Cape Mounted Riflemen, tasked primarily with police work in their respective geographical areas.'<sup id="cite_ref-Dorning_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dorning-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:3</sup> In 1913 and 1914, the new 23,400-member Citizen Force was called on to suppress several industrial strikes on the <a href="/wiki/Witwatersrand" title="Witwatersrand">Witwatersrand</a>.</p>
<p>In accordance with the 1912 Defence Act, the Active Citizen Force was established under Brig. Gen. C.F. Beyers on 1 July 1913.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The authorized strength of the ACF and Coast Garrison Force was 25,155 and by 31 December actual strength stood at 23,462.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_I">World War I</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=South_African_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: World War I">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BL_5.4_inch_Howitzer_and_Crew_East_Africa_WWI.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/BL_5.4_inch_Howitzer_and_Crew_East_Africa_WWI.jpg/250px-BL_5.4_inch_Howitzer_and_Crew_East_Africa_WWI.jpg" width="250" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/BL_5.4_inch_Howitzer_and_Crew_East_Africa_WWI.jpg/375px-BL_5.4_inch_Howitzer_and_Crew_East_Africa_WWI.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/BL_5.4_inch_Howitzer_and_Crew_East_Africa_WWI.jpg/500px-BL_5.4_inch_Howitzer_and_Crew_East_Africa_WWI.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="863" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BL_5.4_inch_Howitzer_and_Crew_East_Africa_WWI.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/BL_5.4_inch_Howitzer" class="mw-redirect" title="BL 5.4 inch Howitzer">BL 5.4 inch Howitzer</a> and crew, <a href="/wiki/East_African_Campaign_(World_War_I)" title="East African Campaign (World War I)">East Africa</a>, 1916 or 1917. Photo courtesy of SANDF Archives, from Nöthling, C J (ed), "Ultima Ratio Regum: Artillery History of South Africa" 1987</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> broke out in 1914, the South African government chose to join the war on the side of the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Allies</a>. General <a href="/wiki/Louis_Botha" title="Louis Botha">Louis Botha</a>, the then prime minister, faced widespread <a href="/wiki/Afrikaner" class="mw-redirect" title="Afrikaner">Afrikaner</a> opposition to fighting alongside Great Britain so soon after the <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Second Boer War</a> and had to put down a revolt by some of the more militant elements before he could send an expeditionary force of some 67,000 troops to invade <a href="/wiki/German_South-West_Africa" title="German South-West Africa">German South-West Africa</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a>). The German troops stationed there eventually surrendered to the South African forces in July 1915. (In 1920 South Africa received a <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> mandate to govern the former German colony and to prepare it for independence within a few years.)</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CAP_BADGE_1ST_SA_INFANTRY_BRIGADE.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/CAP_BADGE_1ST_SA_INFANTRY_BRIGADE.jpg/120px-CAP_BADGE_1ST_SA_INFANTRY_BRIGADE.jpg" width="120" height="117" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/CAP_BADGE_1ST_SA_INFANTRY_BRIGADE.jpg/180px-CAP_BADGE_1ST_SA_INFANTRY_BRIGADE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/CAP_BADGE_1ST_SA_INFANTRY_BRIGADE.jpg/240px-CAP_BADGE_1ST_SA_INFANTRY_BRIGADE.jpg 2x" data-file-width="391" data-file-height="382" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CAP_BADGE_1ST_SA_INFANTRY_BRIGADE.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cap badge of 1st SA Infantry Brigade</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Later, an <a href="/wiki/Infantry" title="Infantry">infantry</a> <a href="/wiki/Brigade" title="Brigade">brigade</a> and various other supporting units were shipped to France in order to fight on the <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Western Front (World War I)">Western Front</a> as the <a href="/wiki/South_African_Overseas_Expeditionary_Force" title="South African Overseas Expeditionary Force">South African Overseas Expeditionary Force</a>. The <a href="/wiki/1st_Infantry_Brigade_(South_Africa)" title="1st Infantry Brigade (South Africa)">1st South African Brigade</a> consisted of four infantry <a href="/wiki/Battalion" title="Battalion">battalions</a>, representing men from all four provinces of the <a href="/wiki/Union_of_South_Africa" title="Union of South Africa">Union of South Africa</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia" title="Rhodesia">Rhodesia</a>: the 1st Regiment was from the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Province" title="Cape Province">Cape Province</a>, the 2nd Regiment was from <a href="/wiki/KwaZulu-Natal_Province" class="mw-redirect" title="KwaZulu-Natal Province">Natal</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Orange_Free_State" title="Orange Free State">Orange Free State</a> and the 3rd Regiment was from <a href="/wiki/Transvaal_Province" title="Transvaal Province">Transvaal</a> and Rhodesia. The 4th Regiment was called the <i>South African Scottish</i> and was raised from members of the <a href="/wiki/Transvaal_Scottish_Regiment" title="Transvaal Scottish Regiment">Transvaal Scottish</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Town_Highlanders_Regiment" title="Cape Town Highlanders Regiment">Cape Town Highlanders</a>; they wore the Atholl Murray <a href="/wiki/Tartan" title="Tartan">tartan</a>.</p>
<p>The supporting units included five <a href="/wiki/Artillery_battery" title="Artillery battery">batteries of heavy artillery</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Ambulance" title="Ambulance">field ambulance</a> unit, a <a href="/wiki/Royal_Engineers" title="Royal Engineers">Royal Engineers</a> signals <a href="/wiki/Company_(military_unit)" title="Company (military unit)">company</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Military_hospital" title="Military hospital">military hospital</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WW1_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WW1-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Delville_Wood_Battle_July_1916.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Delville_Wood_Battle_July_1916.jpg/220px-Delville_Wood_Battle_July_1916.jpg" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Delville_Wood_Battle_July_1916.jpg/330px-Delville_Wood_Battle_July_1916.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Delville_Wood_Battle_July_1916.jpg/440px-Delville_Wood_Battle_July_1916.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3226" data-file-height="2181" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Delville_Wood_Battle_July_1916.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
South Africans and Rhodesians fight the Germans hand-to-hand in <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Delville_Wood" title="Battle of Delville Wood">Delville Wood</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The most costly action that the South African forces on the Western Front fought in was the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Delville_Wood" title="Battle of Delville Wood">Battle of Delville Wood</a> in 1916 – of the 3,000 men from the brigade who entered the wood, only 768 emerged unscathed. Another tragic loss of life for the South African forces during the war was <a href="/wiki/The_Mendi_sinking" class="mw-redirect" title="The Mendi sinking">the Mendi sinking</a> on 21 February 1917, when the troopship Mendi – while transporting 607 members of the <a href="/wiki/South_African_Native_Labour_Corps" title="South African Native Labour Corps">South African Native Labour Corps</a> from Britain to France – was struck and cut almost in half by another ship.</p>
<p>In addition, the war against the German and <a href="/wiki/Askari" title="Askari">Askari</a> forces in <a href="/wiki/German_East_Africa" title="German East Africa">German East Africa</a> also involved more than 20,000 South African troops; they fought under General Jan Smuts's command when he directed the British campaign against there in 1915. (During the war, the army was led by General Smuts, who had rejoined the army from his position as Minister of Defence on the outbreak of the war.)</p>
<p>South Africans also saw action with the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Corps" title="Cape Corps">Cape Corps</a> in <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a>.</p>
<p>More than 146,000 whites, 83,000 blacks and 2,500 people of mixed race ("<a href="/wiki/Coloured" class="mw-redirect" title="Coloured">Coloureds</a>") and <a href="/wiki/Asians_in_South_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Asians in South Africa">Asians</a> served in South African military units during the war, including 43,000 in German South-West Africa and 30,000 on the Western Front. An estimated 3,000 South Africans also joined the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Flying_Corps" title="Royal Flying Corps">Royal Flying Corps</a>.</p>
<p>The total South African casualties during the war was about 18,600 with over 12,452 killed – more than 4,600 in the European theater alone.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interwar_period">Interwar period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=South_African_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Interwar period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Wartime casualties and postwar demobilization weakened the UDF. New legislation in 1922 re-established conscription for white males<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> over the age of 21 for four years of military training and service and re-constituted the Permanent Force. UDF troops assumed internal security tasks in South Africa and quelled several revolts against South African domination in South-West Africa. South Africans suffered high casualties, especially in 1922, when an independent group of <a href="/wiki/Khoikhoi" title="Khoikhoi">Khoikhoi</a> – known as the Bondelswart-<a href="/wiki/Herero_people" title="Herero people">Herero</a> for the black bands that they wore into battle – led one of numerous revolts; in 1925, when a mixed-race population – the <a href="/wiki/Baster" title="Baster">Basters</a> – demanded cultural autonomy and political independence; and in 1932, when the <a href="/wiki/Ovambo_people" title="Ovambo people">Ovambo</a> (Ambo) population along the border with <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a> demanded an end to South African domination. During the Rand strike of 1922, 14,000 members of the ACF and certain A class reservists were called up.<sup id="cite_ref-Dorn8_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dorn8-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Expenditure cuts saw the UDF as a whole reduced. The last remaining regiment of the <a href="/w/index.php?title=South_Africa_Mounted_Riflemen&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="South Africa Mounted Riflemen (page does not exist)">South Africa Mounted Riflemen</a> was disbanded on 31 March 1926 and the number of military districts was reduced from 16 to six on 1 April 1926. The Brigade HQ of the SA Field Artillery was also disbanded.<sup id="cite_ref-Dorn8_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dorn8-10">[10]</a></sup> In 1933 the six military districts were redesignated <a href="/wiki/Command_(military_formation)" title="Command (military formation)">Commands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dorning_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dorning-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:9</sup> As a result of its conscription policies, the UDF increased its active-duty forces to 56,000 by the late 1930s; 100,000 men also belonged to the National Riflemen's Reserve, which provided weapons training and practice.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II">World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=South_African_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/South_African_Army_Order_of_Battle_1940" title="South African Army Order of Battle 1940">South African Army Order of Battle 1940</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:IWM-E-10601-Marmon-Herrington-19420413.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/IWM-E-10601-Marmon-Herrington-19420413.jpg/200px-IWM-E-10601-Marmon-Herrington-19420413.jpg" width="200" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/IWM-E-10601-Marmon-Herrington-19420413.jpg/300px-IWM-E-10601-Marmon-Herrington-19420413.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/32/IWM-E-10601-Marmon-Herrington-19420413.jpg 2x" data-file-width="346" data-file-height="300" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:IWM-E-10601-Marmon-Herrington-19420413.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A South African <a href="/wiki/Marmon-Herrington_Armoured_Car" title="Marmon-Herrington Armoured Car">Marmon-Herrington Armoured Car</a> conducting reconnaissance in North Africa.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During World War II, the South African Army fought in the East African, <a href="/wiki/North_African_Campaign" title="North African Campaign">North African</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italian_Campaign_(World_War_II)" title="Italian Campaign (World War II)">Italian</a> campaigns. In 1939, the army at home in South Africa was divided between a number of regional <a href="/wiki/Command_(military_formation)" title="Command (military formation)">commands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> These included <a href="/wiki/Western_Cape_Command" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Cape Command">Cape Command</a> (with its headquarters at the <a href="/wiki/Castle_of_Good_Hope" title="Castle of Good Hope">Castle of Good Hope</a>, Cape Town), <a href="/wiki/Orange_Free_State_Command" title="Orange Free State Command">Orange Free State Command</a>, <a href="/wiki/Natal_Command" title="Natal Command">Natal Command</a>, <a href="/wiki/Witwatersrand_Command" title="Witwatersrand Command">Witwatersrand Command</a> (5th and 9th Brigades plus the <a href="/wiki/Transvaal_Horse_Artillery" title="Transvaal Horse Artillery">Transvaal Horse Artillery</a>), <a href="/wiki/Robert%27s_Heights" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert's Heights">Robert's Heights</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Transvaal_Command&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Transvaal Command (page does not exist)">Transvaal Command</a> (HQ <a href="/wiki/Robert%27s_Heights" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert's Heights">Robert's Heights</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Province_Command" title="Eastern Province Command">Eastern Province Command</a> at <a href="/wiki/East_London" title="East London">East London</a>.</p>
<p>With the declaration of war in September 1939, the South African Army numbered only 5,353 regulars,<sup id="cite_ref-MilHist115_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MilHist115-12">[12]</a></sup> with an additional 14,631 men of the Active Citizen Force (ACF) which gave peace time training to volunteers and in time of war would form the main body of the army. Pre-war plans did not anticipate that the army would fight outside southern Africa and it was trained and equipped only for bush warfare.</p>
<p>One of the problems to continuously face South Africa during the war was the shortage of available men. Due to its racial policies it would only consider arming men of European descent which limited the available pool of men aged between 20 and 40 to around 320,000. In addition the declaration of war on Germany had the support of only a narrow majority in the South African parliament and was far from universally popular. Indeed, there was a significant minority actively opposed to the war and under these conditions conscription was never an option. The expansion of the army and its deployment overseas depended entirely on volunteers.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/1st_Infantry_Division_(South_Africa)" title="1st Infantry Division (South Africa)">1st South African Infantry Division</a> took part in several actions in East Africa in 1940, North Africa in 1941 and 1942, including the <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_El_Alamein" title="Second Battle of El Alamein">Second Battle of El Alamein</a>, before being withdrawn to South Africa.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/2nd_Infantry_Division_(South_Africa)" title="2nd Infantry Division (South Africa)">2nd South African Infantry Division</a> also took part in a number of actions in North Africa during 1942, but on 21 June 1942 two complete infantry brigades of the division as well as most of the supporting units were captured at the fall of <a href="/wiki/Tobruk" title="Tobruk">Tobruk</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/3rd_Infantry_Division_(South_Africa)" title="3rd Infantry Division (South Africa)">3rd South African Infantry Division</a> never took an active part in any battles but instead organised and trained the South African home defence forces, performed garrison duties and supplied replacements for the South African 1st Infantry Division and the South African 2nd Infantry Division. However, one of this division's constituent brigades – <a href="/w/index.php?title=7th_South_African_Motorised_Brigade&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="7th South African Motorised Brigade (page does not exist)">7th South African Motorised Brigade</a> – did take part in the invasion of <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> in 1942.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/6th_Armoured_Division_(South_Africa)" title="6th Armoured Division (South Africa)">6th South African Armoured Division</a> fought in numerous actions in Italy from 1944 to 1945.</p>
<p>Of the 334,000 men volunteered for full time service in the South African Army during the war (including some 211,000 whites, 77,000 blacks and 46,000 <a href="/wiki/Cape_Coloureds" title="Cape Coloureds">Cape Coloureds</a> and Asians), about 9,000 were killed in action, though the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_War_Graves_Commission" title="Commonwealth War Graves Commission">Commonwealth War Graves Commission</a> has records of 11,023 known South African war dead during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:6_SA_Arm_Div_Monza_Parade.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/6_SA_Arm_Div_Monza_Parade.jpg/250px-6_SA_Arm_Div_Monza_Parade.jpg" width="250" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/6_SA_Arm_Div_Monza_Parade.jpg/375px-6_SA_Arm_Div_Monza_Parade.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/6_SA_Arm_Div_Monza_Parade.jpg/500px-6_SA_Arm_Div_Monza_Parade.jpg 2x" data-file-width="950" data-file-height="658" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:6_SA_Arm_Div_Monza_Parade.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
General <a href="/wiki/Mark_W._Clark" title="Mark W. Clark">Mark W. Clark</a> (15th Army Group) takes the salute from <a href="/wiki/M-10_tank_destroyer" class="mw-redirect" title="M-10 tank destroyer">M-10 tank destroyers</a> of the 11th Armoured Brigade of the <a href="/wiki/6th_South_African_Armoured_Division" class="mw-redirect" title="6th South African Armoured Division">6th South African Armoured Division</a> at the commemoration parade marking the end of hostilities in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>. <a href="/wiki/Autodromo_Nazionale_Monza" title="Autodromo Nazionale Monza">Monza Race Circuit</a>: 14 July 1945</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Postwar_period">Postwar period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=South_African_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Postwar period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Wartime expansion was again followed by rapid demobilization after World War II. By then, a century of Anglo-Boer clashes followed by decades of growing British influence in South Africa had fueled Afrikaner resentment. Resurgent Afrikaner nationalism was an important factor in the growth of the <a href="/wiki/National_Party_(South_Africa)" title="National Party (South Africa)">National Party</a> (NP) as the 1948 elections approached. After the narrow election victory by the NP in 1948, the government began the steady Afrikanerization of the military; it expanded military service obligations and enforced conscription laws more strictly. Most UDF conscripts underwent three months of Citizen Force training in their first year of service, and an additional three weeks of training each year for four years after that.</p>
<p>In 1948, the new Minister of Defence, <a href="/wiki/Frans_Erasmus" title="Frans Erasmus">Frans Erasmus</a>, aimed ' to level the playing-fields' within the Union Defence Force, which was strongly British-oriented in usages, structures, uniforms and nomenclature.<sup id="cite_ref-Steenkamp_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steenkamp-14">[14]</a></sup> This developed from an attempt at affirmative action into a 'politically tinged purge'.</p>
<p>The various Commando units, previously `Skietverenigings`, were later classified as Type A, B or C independent Commandos and continued as single-battalion or small independent units. As part of the post-war reorganization, the Defence Rifle Associations were disbanded in 1948 and replaced by a new Commando organization with a strength of 90,000 men.<sup id="cite_ref-SADR_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SADR-15">[15]</a></sup> At the same time, the Afrikaans-oriented single-battalion regiments founded in 1934 underwent at least one change of name and sometimes more. An early victim was the renowned Middellandse Regiment, which became Regiment Gideon Scheepers in 1954.</p>
<p>It was also decided to establish and maintain two complete army divisions in the UDF: namely <a href="/wiki/1st_Infantry_Division_(South_Africa)" title="1st Infantry Division (South Africa)">1 SA Infantry Division</a> and <a href="/wiki/6th_Armoured_Division_(South_Africa)" title="6th Armoured Division (South Africa)">6 SA Armoured Division</a>, consisting of 1, 2, 3, 12, and 13 (CF) Infantry Brigades and the (PF) <a href="/w/index.php?title=11th_Armoured_Brigade_(South_Africa)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="11th Armoured Brigade (South Africa) (page does not exist)">11th Armoured Brigade</a>. The divisions were formally established with effect from 1 July 1948, but with the exception of 11 Brigade they were disbanded on 1 November 1949, mainly as a result of difficulties in obtaining volunteer recruits to man the Citizen Force brigades. The 11th Armoured Brigade was itself disbanded on 1 October 1953. In the early 1950s the Union undertook, however, to provide one armoured division for active service in the Middle East in the event of war in the region. To this end some 200 <a href="/wiki/Centurion_tank" class="mw-redirect" title="Centurion tank">Centurion tanks</a> were ordered, and the first were delivered in July 1952. During Exercise Oranje, conducted in 1956, the Army trialled its Centurions for the first time in a simulated nuclear war situation.</p>
<p>The Defence Act (No. 44) of 1957 renamed the UDF the <a href="/wiki/South_African_Defence_Force" title="South African Defence Force">South African Defence Force</a> (SADF) and established within it some quick-reaction units, or Commandos, to respond to localized threats. The SADF, numbering about 20,000 in 1958, would grow to almost 80,000 in the next two decades.</p>
<p>In 1960 there was another wave of regimental name-changing.<sup id="cite_ref-Steenkamp_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steenkamp-14">[14]</a></sup> Regiment Gideon Scheepers became Regiment Groot Karoo, and three regiments named after famous Boer generals Regiment De La Rey (given its 13 World War 2 battle honours, the most celebrated of the 1934 battalions), Regiment Louw Wepener and Regiment De Wet were inexplicably renamed Regiment Wes-Transvaal, Regiment Oos-Vrystaat and Regiment Noord-Vrystaat. After strenuous efforts, Regiment Wes-Transvaal, Regiment Oos-Vrystaat and Regiment Noord-Vrystaat regained their honoured names.</p>
<p>Following the declaration of the Republic of South Africa in 1961, the "Royal" title was dropped from the names of army regiments like the <a href="/wiki/Natal_Carbineers" title="Natal Carbineers">Natal Carbineers</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Durban_Light_Infantry" title="Durban Light Infantry">Durban Light Infantry</a>, and the Crown removed from regimental badges.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id=".22Border_War.22_.281966.E2.80.931989.29">"Border War" (1966–1989)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=South_African_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: &quot;Border War&quot; (1966–1989)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the early 1960s, the military threat by the <a href="/wiki/South-West_Africa_People%27s_Organisation" class="mw-redirect" title="South-West Africa People's Organisation">South-West Africa People's Organisation</a> (SWAPO) and its Communist backers in <a href="/wiki/South_West_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="South West Africa">South West Africa</a> prompted the South African government to increase military service obligations and to extend periods of active duty. The Defence Act (No. 12) of 1961 authorized the minister of defense to deploy Citizen Force troops and Commandos for "riot" control, often to quell anti-<a href="/wiki/Apartheid" title="Apartheid">apartheid</a> demonstrations, especially when it deteriorated into mob riots with loss of life. The Defence Act (No. 85) of 1967 also expanded military obligations, requiring white male citizens to perform national service, including an initial period of training, a period of active duty, and several years in reserve status, subject to immediate call-up.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:272px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SADF_Commemorate.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/SADF_Commemorate.jpg/270px-SADF_Commemorate.jpg" width="270" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/SADF_Commemorate.jpg/405px-SADF_Commemorate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/SADF_Commemorate.jpg/540px-SADF_Commemorate.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3504" data-file-height="2336" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SADF_Commemorate.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
SADF Commemorate "Erected in sacred memory of all members of the South African Defence Force who gave their lives in the service of The Republic of South Africa" at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Klapperkop" class="mw-redirect" title="Fort Klapperkop">Fort Klapperkop</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From 1966 to 1989 the SADF, with its <a href="/wiki/South_West_African_Territorial_Force" class="mw-redirect" title="South West African Territorial Force">South West African Territorial Force</a> auxiliary, fought the counter-insurgency <a href="/wiki/South_African_Border_War" title="South African Border War">South African Border War</a> against SWAPO rebels in South-West Africa (Namibia). These operations included the raising of special units such as the <a href="/wiki/South_African_32_Battalion" class="mw-redirect" title="South African 32 Battalion">South African 32 Battalion</a>. They also carried out operations in support of <a href="/wiki/UNITA" title="UNITA">UNITA</a> rebels in Angola and against the Cuban troops that supported the Angolan government.</p>
<p>As far as conventional formations were concerned, <a href="/wiki/7_South_African_Infantry_Division" title="7 South African Infantry Division">7 SA Division</a> and 17, 18 and 19 Brigades were established on 1 April 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-Dorning_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dorning-6">[6]</a></sup> Difficulties with manning levels saw the disestablishment of 7 SA Division on 1 November 1967 and its replacement by the Army Task Force (HQ) and 16 Brigade.</p>
<p>Also during the 1970s, the SADF began accepting "non-whites" and women into the military as career soldiers, not only as temporary volunteers or reservists; however, the former served mostly, if not exclusively, in segregated units while the latter were not assigned to combat roles. By the end of the 1970s, the South African military was increasingly called upon to confront external threats and internal unrest which started escalating to armed confrontation between the South African state and the liberation forces. Principal among these armed groups was that of the ANC's Umkhonto we Sizwe , the AZAPO's Azanian People's Liberation Army and the PAC's Poqo.</p>
<p>In 1973 two new infantry units were established: <a href="/wiki/7_South_African_Infantry_Battalion" title="7 South African Infantry Battalion">7 South African Infantry Battalion</a> (Bourke's Luck) and <a href="/wiki/8_South_African_Infantry_Battalion" title="8 South African Infantry Battalion">8 SA Infantry Battalion</a> (Upington), as well as <a href="/w/index.php?title=11_Commando_(Kimberley)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="11 Commando (Kimberley) (page does not exist)">11 Commando (Kimberley)</a>, which to a great extent took over the functions of the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Danie_Theron_Combat_School&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Danie Theron Combat School (page does not exist)">Danie Theron Combat School</a>'s training wing. In 1973 the SADF also took over responsibility for the defence of <a href="/wiki/South_West_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="South West Africa">South West Africa</a> (today Namibia) from the South African Police. During the succeeding months the Army became involved in combat operations for the first time since the Second World War, clashing with groups of SWAPO infiltrating into South West Africa.</p>

<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">John Keegan, World Armies, p.639</cite></div>
<p>From 1 September 1972 Army Task Force Headquarters was redesignated HQ <a href="/wiki/7_South_African_Infantry_Division" title="7 South African Infantry Division">7 South African Infantry Division</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dorning_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dorning-6">[6]</a></sup> Two years later, it was decided to organize the Army's conventional force into two divisions under a corps headquarters. Both were primarily reserve (Citizen Force) formations, though the division and brigade HQs were Permanent Force. The headquarters of the two divisions were established on 1 August 1974, and 8th Armoured Division was active at its headquarters at <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lord%27s_Grounds&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Lord's Grounds (page does not exist)">Lord's Grounds</a>, Durban, until at least 27 September 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> 1 SA Corps itself was established in August 1974 and was active until 30 January 1977.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> It appears from Colonel Lionel Crook's book on 71 Brigade<sup id="cite_ref-Crook71Bge_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crook71Bge-19">[19]</a></sup> that four of the six brigades were redesignations of 16th, 17th, 18th, and 19th Brigades. 71 Motorised Brigade was the former 17 Brigade, 72 Brigade was the former 18 Brigade, 73 Brigade was a new formation, 81 Brigade was the former 16 Brigade, 82 Brigade was the former 19 Brigade, and 84 Brigade was new.<sup id="cite_ref-Crook71Bge_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crook71Bge-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:2</sup></p>
<p>In the early 1980s, the Army was restructured in order to counter all forms of insurgency while at the same time maintaining a credible conventional force. To meet these requirements, the Army was subdivided into conventional and counterinsurgency forces. The counterinsurgency forces were further divided into nine territorial commands, each of which was responsible to the Chief of the Army. This force consisted of members of the Permanent Force, Commandos, and a few selected Citizens Force units. The Citizen Force, through the 7th and 8th Divisions, provided the conventional defence force. By July 1987 the number of territorial commands was expanded to ten, and the Walvis Bay military area was often counted as an eleventh.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The commands were the <a href="/wiki/Western_Province_Command" title="Western Province Command">Western Province Command</a> (HQ <a href="/wiki/Cape_Town" title="Cape Town">Cape Town</a>, 1959-1998); <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Province_Command" title="Eastern Province Command">Eastern Province Command</a> (HQ Port Elizabeth, 1959-1998); <a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_Cape_Command&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Northern Cape Command (page does not exist)">Northern Cape Command</a> (HQ <a href="/wiki/Kimberley,_Northern_Cape" title="Kimberley, Northern Cape">Kimberley</a>); <a href="/wiki/Orange_Free_State_Command" title="Orange Free State Command">Orange Free State Command</a> (HQ Bloemfontein, 1959-1998); <a href="/wiki/Northern_Transvaal_Command" title="Northern Transvaal Command">Northern Transvaal Command</a> (HQ <a href="/wiki/Pretoria" title="Pretoria">Pretoria</a>); <a href="/wiki/Witwatersrand_Command" title="Witwatersrand Command">Witwatersrand Command</a> (HQ Johannesburg, subject of a bombing in 1987);<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> <a href="/w/index.php?title=Northwestern_Command&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Northwestern Command (page does not exist)">Northwestern Command</a> (HQ <a href="/wiki/Potchefstroom" title="Potchefstroom">Potchefstroom</a>); <a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Transvaal_Command&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Eastern Transvaal Command (page does not exist)">Eastern Transvaal Command</a> (HQ <a href="/wiki/Nelspruit" title="Nelspruit">Nelspruit</a>); <a href="/wiki/Natal_Command" title="Natal Command">Natal Command</a> (Durban), and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Far_North_Command&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Far North Command (page does not exist)">Far North Command</a> (HQ <a href="/wiki/Pietersburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Pietersburg">Pietersburg</a>, which in late 1993 and early 1994 included <a href="/w/index.php?title=Regiment_Hillcrest&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Regiment Hillcrest (page does not exist)">Regiment Hillcrest</a> which was then part of <a href="/w/index.php?title=73_Motorised_Brigade_(South_Africa)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="73 Motorised Brigade (South Africa) (page does not exist)">73 Motorised Brigade</a>, and 73 Brigade itself). The part-time force also operated in the military area of <a href="/wiki/Walvis_Bay" title="Walvis Bay">Walvis Bay</a>.</p>
<p>During this same period, the Engineers and Signals were grouped into the first of the 'type' formations, the <a href="/wiki/South_African_Army_Engineer_Formation" title="South African Army Engineer Formation">South African Army Engineer Formation</a> (in 1982) and the <a href="/wiki/South_African_Army_Signals_Formation" class="mw-redirect" title="South African Army Signals Formation">South African Army Signals Formation</a> (in 1984). Both these formations were made directly responsible to Chief of Army.</p>
<p>In 1984 <a href="/wiki/Northern_Transvaal_Command" title="Northern Transvaal Command">Northern Transvaal Command</a> was subdivided and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Transvaal_Command&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Eastern Transvaal Command (page does not exist)">Eastern Transvaal Command</a> (Nelspruit) and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Far_North_Command&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Far North Command (page does not exist)">Far North Command</a> (Pietersburg) formed. The two new Commands were regarded as theatres and as such also had responsibility for conventional operations (and units) within their areas.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> For example, Far North Command had <a href="/w/index.php?title=73_Motorised_Brigade_(South_Africa)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="73 Motorised Brigade (South Africa) (page does not exist)">73 Motorised Brigade</a> within its area. <a href="/w/index.php?title=Southern_Cape_Command&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Southern Cape Command (page does not exist)">Southern Cape Command</a> may have been disbanded, and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_Cape_Command&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Northern Cape Command (page does not exist)">Northern Cape Command</a> established, in 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> In 1989 the <a href="/wiki/Rand_Light_Infantry" title="Rand Light Infantry">RLI</a> became the conventional reserve for <a href="/w/index.php?title=Far_North_Command&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Far North Command (page does not exist)">Far North Command</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Area_of_responsibility" title="Area of responsibility">area of responsibility</a> of each commands followed the boundaries of the Economic Development Regions.<sup id="cite_ref-ISSSADR_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSSADR-24">[24]</a></sup> Before the dissolution of the territorial commands General <a href="/wiki/Derrick_Mgwebi" title="Derrick Mgwebi">Derrick Mgwebi</a> is also reported to have headed <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mpumalanga_Command&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Mpumalanga Command (page does not exist)">Mpumalanga Command</a>.</p>
<p>During the 1980s, the legal requirements for national service were to register for service at age sixteen and to report for duty when called up, which usually occurred at some time after a man's eighteenth birthday or on leaving school.<sup id="cite_ref-LCEarlyMil_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LCEarlyMil-5">[5]</a></sup> National service obligations could be fulfilled by active-duty military service for two years and by serving in the reserves, generally for ten or twelve years. Reservists generally underwent fifty days per year of active duty or training, after their initial period of service. The system was for the most part that the National Service requirement was for 720 days (two years) and subsequent reserve duty was a further 720 days. The reserve duty was broken up depending on the needs of the units and of the individual concerned. This generally worked out as a ninety-day "operational" commitment one year, followed the next year by a thirty-day commitment in addition to any courses, parades or admin evenings that might be required. Members of the Reserve were able to volunteer for further duty in addition to that mandated. This additional, voluntary, service was recognised with the award of the Emblem for Voluntary Service (EVS) (now the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Badge_for_Reserve_Voluntary_Service&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Badge for Reserve Voluntary Service (page does not exist)">Badge for Reserve Voluntary Service</a> (BRVS)) for five years of voluntary service over and above the mandated commitment. The requirements for national service changed several times during the 1980s and the early 1990s in response to national security needs, and they were suspended in 1993.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-1994">Post-1994</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=South_African_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Post-1994">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:272px;"><a href="/wiki/File:South_African_troops_MONUSCO.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/South_African_troops_MONUSCO.jpg/270px-South_African_troops_MONUSCO.jpg" width="270" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/South_African_troops_MONUSCO.jpg/405px-South_African_troops_MONUSCO.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/South_African_troops_MONUSCO.jpg/540px-South_African_troops_MONUSCO.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5760" data-file-height="3840" /></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:272px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ratel_90_armyrecognition_South-Africa_008.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Ratel_90_armyrecognition_South-Africa_008.jpg/270px-Ratel_90_armyrecognition_South-Africa_008.jpg" width="270" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Ratel_90_armyrecognition_South-Africa_008.jpg/405px-Ratel_90_armyrecognition_South-Africa_008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Ratel_90_armyrecognition_South-Africa_008.jpg/540px-Ratel_90_armyrecognition_South-Africa_008.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="683" /></a>
